  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_16741064-b607-4818-ac56-b6c3de5e175a'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText15483342777154247972" style="display:none">The Jungle Book key characters are Mowgli, a boy raised by wolves and Sher Khan, biggest tiger in India. As Baloo the sleepy brown bear, Bagheera the cunning black panther, Kaa the python, and his other animal friends teach their beloved “man-cub” the ways of the jungle, Mowgli gains the strength and wisdom he needs for his frightful fight with Shere Khan, the tiger who robbed him of his human family. But there are also the tales of Rikki-tikki-tavi the mongoose and his “great war” against the vicious cobras Nag and Nagaina; of Toomai, who watches the elephants dance; and of Kotick the white seal, who swims in the Bering Sea.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/944029-i-don-t-understand-why-it-had-two-separate">I don&#39;t understand why it had two separate stories in it. Was the second part relevant in any way to the first?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_944029'><a id="like_count_community_question_944029" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/944029?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F77270.The_Jungle_Book">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/944029-i-don-t-understand-why-it-had-two-separate">3 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/944029-i-don-t-understand-why-it-had-two-separate">See all 2 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/3082732-r"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_100x100-c7bfe730633c9983e27caaa66a8e244a.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/3082732-r">R.</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">There are a lot more than two. The first three stories are related to Mowgli, and then you have The White Seal, Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, Tomai of the Elephan<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">There are a lot more than two. The first three stories are related to Mowgli, and then you have The White Seal, Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, Tomai of the Elephants, and Her Majesty's Servants (at least in the version of the book I have). There is a song at the end of each story, so seven total stories and seven songs. <br /><br />To tell you the truth, I don't understand why The White Seal is included in the collection, but the rest are related to the jungle in some way--not to each other.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_2124049892" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2124049892" />
    <a title="Luca Ambrosino" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53478239-luca-ambrosino"><img alt="Luca Ambrosino" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523134271p2/53478239.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2124049892?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luca Ambrosino" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luca Ambrosino" href="/user/show/53478239-luca-ambrosino">Luca Ambrosino</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788858016459" href="/book/show/36137838-il-libro-della-giungla">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2124049892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13472784754398045278">English (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77270.The_Jungle_Book' title='The Jungle Book by Rudyard Kipling' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Jungle Book</a>) / Italiano<br/><br/><p>I didn't know that "The Jungle Book" was a collection of tales. Thanks to the Disney movie, I had always identified this novel with the story of Mowgli, the man cub raised by a pack of wolves. But another bedtime reading to my daughter makes me discover that this book is made up of seven stories, and only three of them tell about Mowgli ("Mowgli's Brothers", "Kaa's Hunting" and "Tiger! Tiger!"). The search for a place to live safe from hunters is told in "The </p></span>
  <span id="freeText13472784754398045278" style="display:none">English (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77270.The_Jungle_Book" title="The Jungle Book by Rudyard Kipling" rel="nofollow noopener">The Jungle Book</a>) / Italiano<br><br><p>I didn't know that "The Jungle Book" was a collection of tales. Thanks to the Disney movie, I had always identified this novel with the story of Mowgli, the man cub raised by a pack of wolves. But another bedtime reading to my daughter makes me discover that this book is made up of seven stories, and only three of them tell about Mowgli ("Mowgli's Brothers", "Kaa's Hunting" and "Tiger! Tiger!"). The search for a place to live safe from hunters is told in "The White Seal". "Rikki-Tikki-Tavi" presents us with a nice domesticated mongoose. "Toomai of the Elephants" tells the story of a child with great talent. Finally, an unusual night meeting is told in "Her Majesty's Servants". And to the well-known characters of Bagheera, Baloo, Kaa, Shere Khan and Akela, are added Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, Nag, Nagaina, Kotick, Sea Catch, Billy the mule and many others.</p><p>Probably this is not a maserpiece, anyway Kipling produces an enjoyable read. "The Jungle Book", or the untamed beauty of wildlife.</p><p>Vote: 7</p><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1508422877i/24212085._SX540_.jpg" width="420" height="315" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><p>Non sapevo che "Il Libro della Giungla" fosse una raccolta di racconti, grazie alla Disney lo avevo sempre identificato con la storia di Mowgli, il cucciolo di uomo che viene allevato da un branco di lupi. Invece l'ennesima lettura della buonanotte a mia figlia mi fa scoprire che questo libro è fatto da sette racconti, di cui solo tre narrano le vicende di Mowgli ("I Fratelli di Mowgli", "La Caccia di Kaa" e "Tigre! Tigre!"). La ricerca di un luogo in cui vivere al sicuro dai cacciatori è raccontata in "La Foca Bianca". "Rikki-Tikki-Tavi" ci presenta una simpatica mangusta addomesticata. "Toomai degli Elefanti" racconta le vicende di un bambino con un grande talento. Infine un'insolita riunione notturna è raccontata in "I Servitori della Regina". Ed ai noti personaggi di Bagheera, Baloo, Kaa, Shere Khan e Akela, si aggiungono Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, Nag, Nagaina, Kotick, Sea Catch, Billy il mulo e tanti altri.</p><p>Probabilmente non un capolavoro, ma Kipling produce comunque una lettura piacevole. Il libro della giungla, ovvero la bellezza selvaggia degli animali.</p><p>Voto: 7</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="13472784754398045278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_46702024" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46702024" />
    <a title="Kenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny"><img alt="Kenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472073986p2/1642452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46702024?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenny" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny">Kenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781402743405" href="/book/show/1818248.The_Jungle_Book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=short-stories-novellas">short-stories-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=brits">brits</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=long-haul-reads">long-haul-reads</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46702024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3510150894944722441"><b><i>“For the strength of the Pack is the Wolf, and the strength of the Wolf is the Pack.”</i><br/>The Jungle Book ~~ Rudyard Kipling</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486705982i/21942311._SX540_.jpg' alt='1' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Kipling, Mowgli, Baloo and I have been on a long journey together. I started The Jungle Book nearly ten years ago. I lost my copy when I moved from the west coast to Chicago, found it again, picked up where I left off, lost it again when I moved to Wisconsin, and found it again in 2014. Since then, it's lingered on my nightstand waiting for me to join Mowgli again on his jungle </span>
  <span id="freeText3510150894944722441" style="display:none"><b><i>“For the strength of the Pack is the Wolf, and the strength of the Wolf is the Pack.”</i><br>The Jungle Book ~~ Rudyard Kipling</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486705982i/21942311._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Kipling, Mowgli, Baloo and I have been on a long journey together. I started The Jungle Book nearly ten years ago. I lost my copy when I moved from the west coast to Chicago, found it again, picked up where I left off, lost it again when I moved to Wisconsin, and found it again in 2014. Since then, it's lingered on my nightstand waiting for me to join Mowgli again on his jungle adventures until last week. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486705982i/21942312._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>These stories are absolutely beautiful. It comes as no surprise that Kipling considered himself a poet first, for these stories are truly poetic. Kipling’s writing style is beautifully lyrical. His language flows with a whimsicalness. Reading these stories, I felt that Kipling appreciated the sound of language as much as its ability to convey a message. One thing I must clear up is the perception that this is a children’s book. Disney be damned, this is not a children’s book. It is ideal for young adults, but this is no children’s book.<br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486705982i/21942313._SY540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>From the start, I felt I really lived this book. Mowgli and I ran through the jungle together with our fellow wolf cubs to listen to Akela at the Council Rock. Bagheera and Baloo became my mentors and Kaa my trusted friend. <br> <br>Kipling's magic was always rooted in the reality of common life. All over India there were tales of a child reared by a wolf pack. In the background to the stories, village life goes on with its lazy rhythms – buffalo wading through the shallows, women going to the well, and the man pack exploring the jungle. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486705982i/21942314._SY540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I loved the non-jungle stories as well:<br><br>The White Seal -- Kotick, a rare white-furred northern fur seal, searches for a new home for his people, where they will not be hunted by humans. <br><br>Rikki-Tikki-Tavi -- Rikki-Tikki the mongoose defends a human family living in India against a pair of cobras.<br><br>Toomai of the Elephants -- Toomai, a ten-year-old boy who helps to tend working elephants, is told that he will never be a full-fledged elephant-handler until he has seen the elephants dance. <br><br>The Miracle of Purun Bhagat -- An influential Indian politician abandons his worldly goods to become an ascetic holy man. Later, he must save a village from a landslide with the help of the local animals whom he has befriended.<br><br>Quiquern -- A teenaged Inuit boy and girl set out across the arctic ice on a desperate hunt for food to save their tribe from starvation, guided by the mysterious animal-spirit Quiquern. However, Quiquern is not what he seems.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486705982i/21942315._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The Jungle Books, yes, there are two, were my first journey with Kipling, but they will not be my last. I’m excited to venture back to India with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6989.Rudyard_Kipling" rel="nofollow noopener"> Rudyard Kipling </a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2245153.Kim" rel="nofollow noopener">Kim</a>. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486705982i/21942316._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="3510150894944722441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_287895938" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/287895938" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/287895938?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/1496346.The_Jungle_Book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=why-not-call-it-poetry">why-not-call-it-poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer287895938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17694400192255673164">We are the masters of our planet, but we are not very good masters. We are, in the blunt phrase I saw a zoologist use the other day, a plague species. Sometimes, one feels the world would be better off without human beings. This isn't necessarily a counsel of despair or treachery. Our true loyalty should be not to mankind but to our genes, and most of those genes are to be found in other species who are far less destructive. It would almost be a relief if the beautiful and savage animals we shar</span>
  <span id="freeText17694400192255673164" style="display:none">We are the masters of our planet, but we are not very good masters. We are, in the blunt phrase I saw a zoologist use the other day, a plague species. Sometimes, one feels the world would be better off without human beings. This isn't necessarily a counsel of despair or treachery. Our true loyalty should be not to mankind but to our genes, and most of those genes are to be found in other species who are far less destructive. It would almost be a relief if the beautiful and savage animals we share the world with could take it back from us and relieve us of this responsibility we are not equal to.<br /><br />Oddly enough, the author I know who is best at giving a voice to these feelings is Rudyard Kipling, in his short story <i>Letting in the Jungle</i>. The villagers have angered Mowgli and his friends. Now, the jungle folk return in force, led by Hathi the elephant and his three terrible sons. The ending and the concluding poem are unforgettable:<blockquote>The four pushed side by side; the outer wall bulged, split, and fell, and the villagers, dumb with horror, saw the savage, clay-streaked heads of the wreckers in the ragged gap. Then they fled, houseless and foodless, down the valley, as their village, shredded and tossed and trampled, melted behind them.<br /><br />A month later the place was a dimpled mound, covered with soft, green young stuff; and by the end of the Rains there was the roaring jungle in full blast on the spot that had been under plough not six months before.<br /><br /><b>MOWGLI'S SONG AGAINST PEOPLE</b><br /><i><br /> I will let loose against you the fleet-footed vines--<br /> I will call in the Jungle to stamp out your lines!<br /> The roofs shall fade before it,<br /> The house-beams shall fall,<br /> And the Karela, the bitter Karela,<br /> Shall cover it all!<br /><br /> In the gates of these your councils my people shall sing,<br /> In the doors of these your garners the Bat-folk shall cling;<br /> And the snake shall be your watchman,<br /> By a hearthstone unswept;<br /> For the Karela, the bitter Karela,<br /> Shall fruit where ye slept!<br /><br /> Ye shall not see my strikers; ye shall hear them and guess;<br /> By night, before the moon-rise, I will send for my cess,<br /> And the wolf shall be your herdsman<br /> By a landmark removed,<br /> For the Karela, the bitter Karela,<br /> Shall seed where ye loved!<br /><br /> I will reap your fields before you at the hands of a host;<br /> Ye shall glean behind my reapers, for the bread that is lost,<br /> And the deer shall be your oxen<br /> By a headland untilled,<br /> For the Karela, the bitter Karela,<br /> Shall leaf where ye build!<br /><br /> I have untied against you the club-footed vines,<br /> I have sent in the Jungle to swamp out your lines.<br /> The trees--the trees are on you!<br /> The house-beams shall fall,<br /> And the Karela, the bitter Karela,<br /> Shall cover you all!</i></blockquote>__________________________________<br /><br />Appalled by the dreadful things I was reading in Oreskes's and Conway's <i>Merchants of Doubt</i>, I suggested to a friend the other day that it might be interesting to start a political party called Exterminate Humanity. XH would have a simple agenda: using only legal means, it would support all initiatives which showed promise as possible ways to make human beings extinct. It would for example try to block funding of renewable energy, maximize production of greenhouse gasses, push for increased nuclear arsenals and discourage investment in SpaceGuard and other anti-meteorite defenses. <br /><br />My suggestion was meant ironically, so I was rather disquieted by my friend's reaction. She considered it for a moment, then nodded. "Yes," she said thoughtfully. "Sounds like quite a good idea." But maybe she just wanted to show that Australians could be more ironic than Europeans.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17694400192255673164" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/213735735?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6237864-nandakishore-varma?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6237864-nandakishore-varma?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213735735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8602977913489351617">IMO, Rudyard Kipling is the worst example of the quintessential British Imperialist and Colonialist. His attitude towards India is contemptuous and condescending. As a person, I dislike him intensely.<br/><br/>Kipling writes beautifully. His stories are simple, engaging and profound at the same time. As a writer, I love him.<br/><br/>This is a childhood favourite. I read it first in translation, and then in the original. This is a true classic - it works for one as a child as well as an adult.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1405431310" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1405431310" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1405431310?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1405431310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15633380239024976950"><i>“Welcome to the jungle<br/>We've got fun 'n' games<br/>We got everything you want<br/>Honey, we know the names”</i><br/><br/>The opening poem of <i>The Jungle Book</i>: “Now Rann the Kite brings home the night” etc. is much more elegant than Axl Rose’s effort, but I feel it would be much nicer for you to read it in the context of the book.<br/><br/>Now if you are looking for a review from someone with an in-depth knowledge of Rudyard Kipling’s works you had better look elsewhere. My Kipling-fu is so feeble I did not even know <i>The Jungle Boo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText15633380239024976950" style="display:none"><i>“Welcome to the jungle<br />We've got fun 'n' games<br />We got everything you want<br />Honey, we know the names”</i><br /><br />The opening poem of <i>The Jungle Book</i>: “Now Rann the Kite brings home the night” etc. is much more elegant than Axl Rose’s effort, but I feel it would be much nicer for you to read it in the context of the book.<br /><br />Now if you are looking for a review from someone with an in-depth knowledge of Rudyard Kipling’s works you had better look elsewhere. My Kipling-fu is so feeble I did not even know <i>The Jungle Book</i> was an anthology, not a novel about a badass little boy who blazed a trail for Tarzan. It didn’t even occur to me to read this book until I saw the trailer for the new 2015 movie a few days ago.<br /><br />I’m just going to run through the list of the stories then:<br /><br />1. <b>Mowgli's Brothers</b><br />This surprised me, it’s basically the entire story of Mowgli as I know it from the movies (animated and live action). I wonder if Shere Khan is the inspiration for Chaka Khan? (cue eye rolls). If you only read one story from this book (what a silly notion) read this one.<br /><br />2. <b>Hunting-Song of the Seeonee Pack</b><br />Nice poem, all the poems in this book are nice.<br /><br />3. <b>Kaa's Hunting</b><br />This goes back up the timeline from the conclusion of “Mowgli's Brothers”, it features Mowgli being kidnaped by monkeys. At no point does Mowgli say “<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Cdmqn9JIuzc" rel="nofollow noopener">Take your stinking paws off me you damn dirty ape!</a>”, Kipling was not into pop culture references (I am). Any way, Mowgli is chiefly aided by Kaa the python and his very particular set of skills. <br /><i>“Trust in me”.<br />"but I didn't expect a Spanish Inquisition!"</i><br /><br />4. <b>Road-Song of the Bandar-Log</b><br />Nice poem<br /><br />5. <b>“Tiger! Tiger!”</b><br />Mowgli has a rematch with Shere Khan and finds human society not to his liking, the beds especially (I don’t blame him).<br /><br />6. <b>Mowgli's Song</b><br />Great song, especially the guitar solo.<br /><br />7. <b>The White Seal</b><br />Kotick the white seal is like the Columbus among seals. Very good story. Especially when Kotick decides he has had enough of the ignorant seals and their jibes then proceeds to hand their asses to them. They did not know he has been working out with some marathon swimming.<br /><br />8. <b>Lukannon</b><br />“A sort of very sad seal National Anthem”. Thank you Literaturepage.com<br /><br />9. <b>“Rikki-Tikki-Tavi”</b><br />Laugh at his silly name at your own peril Rikki-tikki-tavi is one badass mofo of a mongoose. I guess he could be the prototype for Ninja Turtles, K9*, Hong Kong Phooey and other superhero animals.<br /><br />10. <b>Darzee's Chant</b><br />Darzee is a tailorbird from Rikki-tikki-tavi’s story with a penchant of breaking into songs at the most inappropriate moment. After Rikki-tikki-tavi is allowed to knock himself out with full blown hero worship through this song.<br /><br />11. <b>Toomai of the Elephants</b><br />An Indian boy takes a clandestine ride on an elephant’s back and witnesses a huge herd of elephants performing “We Will Rock You”. <br /><i>“You got blood on yo' face<br />You big disgrace<br />Wavin' your trunk all over the place”</i><br /><br />12. <b>Shiv and the Grasshopper</b><br />“The song that Toomai's mother sang to the baby”<br /><br />13. <b>Her Majesty's Servants</b><br />Ah! Oh dear! For me this story is like throwing an eel at a marble wall, it just won’t stick. I listened** to it twice and I still can’t remember what it’s about. Somethihg to do with a bunch of animals nattering about something completely devoid of interest.<br /><br />14. <b>Parade Song of the Camp Animals</b><br />Related to the previous story. No thanks.<br /><br />That’s it then, I enjoyed most of the stories, poems and song, except number 13 and 14 as mentioned above.<br /><br />Definitely recommended, especially the first story, which is a bear necessity.<br /><br />_____________________________________<br />* Hey Cecily, I managed to sneak one in!<br /><br />** <a href="https://librivox.org/the-jungle-book-by-rudyard-kipling-2/" rel="nofollow noopener">Librivox Audiobook</a>, very nicely read/performed by Phil Chenevert. Thank you!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15633380239024976950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2550005334" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2550005334" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63168028-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516912354p2/63168028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2550005334?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/63168028-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/34733462-the-jungle-book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2550005334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3201118820836440616"><b>India</b><br/>The illustrated Jungle Book is a mixed bag of positives and negatives, and if your only experience of the story is from the films then you are in for a surprising revelation. Firstly the Jungle Book is not one story but an anthology of 7 short stories and 7 songs, and understandably with some more appealing than others. The contents include:<br/><br/>Mowgli’s Brothers (Story)<br/>Hunting-Song of the Seeonee Pack (Song)<br/>Kaa’s Hunting (Story)<br/>Road-Song of the Bandar-Log (Song)<br/>“Tiger! Tiger!” (Story)<br/>Mowgli’s S</span>
  <span id="freeText3201118820836440616" style="display:none"><b>India</b><br />The illustrated Jungle Book is a mixed bag of positives and negatives, and if your only experience of the story is from the films then you are in for a surprising revelation. Firstly the Jungle Book is not one story but an anthology of 7 short stories and 7 songs, and understandably with some more appealing than others. The contents include:<br /><br />Mowgli’s Brothers (Story)<br />Hunting-Song of the Seeonee Pack (Song)<br />Kaa’s Hunting (Story)<br />Road-Song of the Bandar-Log (Song)<br />“Tiger! Tiger!” (Story)<br />Mowgli’s Song (Song)<br />The White Seal (Story)<br />Lukannon (Song)<br />“Rikki-Tikki-Tavi” (Story)<br />Darzee’s Chant (Song)<br />Toomai of the Elephants (Story)<br />Shiv and the Grasshopper (Song)<br />Her Majesty’s Servants (Story)<br />Parade-Song of the Camp Animals (Song)<br /><br />This is also not a child’s book, it is dark, threatening and violent. Even Baloo while teaching Mowgli the ways and language of the jungle, leaves him heavily bruised. The general story, we all know, as Mowgli the Man-Cub (the Frog) is found as an infant and reared by wolves, taught and watched over by Baloo and Bagheera, and hunted by Shere Khan. However, if you’ve only seen the films and are unaware of the book, then expect a few surprises. The role of characters are transformed, interactions are altered and plots are changed. Death is a typical outcome, often clinical and ruthless, but with a purpose. The written narrative and dialogue from Rudyard Kipling reminds us just how great a writer he is, how he constructs a layered storyline and uses such lyrical prose to describe the scene and activities. Each story starts with a little poetic verse that magically blends with the story.<br /><br />Only the first 3 stories relate to Mowgli, the others are a seal, mongoose, elephants and the ensemble of animals in Her Majesty’s Servants. This is an illustrated version with two types of images; black and white sketch which are exceptionally well drawn, and full-colour prints that seem to vary in quality. This is a Kindle version and the formatting with the images is really poor and inconsistent, I actually can't believe how poor the formatting is.<br /><br />I wasn’t quite sure with this and would probably rate it more accurately as 3.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3201118820836440616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_552688694" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/552688694" />
    <a title="Lynne King" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15736557-lynne-king"><img alt="Lynne King" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357126217p2/15736557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/552688694?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynne King" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynne King" href="/user/show/15736557-lynne-king">Lynne King</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788497890540" href="/book/show/6901705-the-jungle-book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15736557-lynne-king?shelf=children-s-classics">children-s-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15736557-lynne-king?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer552688694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11899201203532844023">Two weeks ago I arrived at Aberdovey, a small seaside village on the Dyfi estuary in west Wales. So to discover sun, and thus an ideal day for the beach, I needed a book. Having no luck finding a bookshop, and minus my Kindle, the young man in the pub mentioned that the RNLI were selling books. So when I saw the smiling face of Mowgli on the cover of “The Jungle Book”, well I had to purchase it and also support the Lifeboats as they do such marvellous work.<br/><br/>I had read this collection of short sto</span>
  <span id="freeText11899201203532844023" style="display:none">Two weeks ago I arrived at Aberdovey, a small seaside village on the Dyfi estuary in west Wales. So to discover sun, and thus an ideal day for the beach, I needed a book. Having no luck finding a bookshop, and minus my Kindle, the young man in the pub mentioned that the RNLI were selling books. So when I saw the smiling face of Mowgli on the cover of “The Jungle Book”, well I had to purchase it and also support the Lifeboats as they do such marvellous work.<br /><br />I had read this collection of short stories as a child but had forgotten the names and the animals and so what a delight it was to reread them.<br /><br />Who cannot possibly be excited and enthralled by the adventures of Mowgli (who was also known as Frog), a young abandoned man cub, who wanders into a wolf’s den in the Indian jungle? As this is fiction, he would, of course, not be eaten by its occupants but I was somewhat astonished by the child’s age and what he was able to do:<br /><br />“Directly in front of him (Father Wolf), holding on by a low branch, stood a naked brown baby who could just walk, as soft and as dimpled a little thing as ever came to a wolf’s cave at night. He looked up into Father Wolf’s face and laughed.”<br /><br />How could the wolves not possibly love him and welcome him into the pack? But still there were struggles for Mowgli from members of the pack; however, with the help of such staunch friends as Bagheera, the panther, and Baloo the bear, he managed to overcome them. When Mowgli is kidnapped by the Monkey People (the Bandar-log), Bagheera and Baloo enlist the help of a rather villainous Rock Python called Kaa as the monkies were terrified of him; I was too and could that snake move. Phew…<br /><br />What is sad though, despite the child learning all about the laws and languages of the jungle, Mowgli was never really accepted by the wolves (apart from his adopted parents), nor human beings either, when he was forced to leave the jungle and go and live and work in a local village. Sadly, he was betwixt and between. Also, did I miss something in that he remained naked all the time? That could have caused serious problems surely in the jungle, especially?<br /><br />Out of the other short stories, I definitely preferred “The White Seal” and Kotick the seal that at birth was white and caused great amazement to his parents:<br /><br />“Sea Catch,” Matkah said at last, “our baby’s going to be white!”<br />“Empty clam-shells and dry seaweed!” snorted Sea Catch. “There never has been such a thing in the world as a white seal.”<br />“I can’t help that,” said Matkah, “there’s going to be now”.<br /><br />And Kotick would indeed benefit from being white. What fun that was to read.<br /><br />I’m so pleased that I discovered this little gem of a book. Also to think that it was first published in 1894 and still gives immense pleasure to its readers, as can be seen by its place on Amazon’s best sellers’ list in the US. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11899201203532844023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3498390143" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3498390143" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3498390143?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007350858" href="/book/show/8829005-the-jungle-book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=brittish-lit">brittish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=children-young-adult">children-young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3498390143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5307800589527439456">I first read <i> The Jungle Book </i> at a very young age that I can hardly remember any of it. The Disney movie helped me to recall some of the adventures of Mowgli, but the book was not too familiar for me. This reading, therefore, was as new as the first time.<br/><br/>The common understanding of <i> The Jungle Book </i> is that it is the story of Mowgli and his adventures, his friendship with Bagheera, the black panther, and the wolf pack, and his enmity with Shere Khan, the tiger. But surprisingly it is not so. It</span>
  <span id="freeText5307800589527439456" style="display:none">I first read <i> The Jungle Book </i> at a very young age that I can hardly remember any of it. The Disney movie helped me to recall some of the adventures of Mowgli, but the book was not too familiar for me. This reading, therefore, was as new as the first time.<br /><br />The common understanding of <i> The Jungle Book </i> is that it is the story of Mowgli and his adventures, his friendship with Bagheera, the black panther, and the wolf pack, and his enmity with Shere Khan, the tiger. But surprisingly it is not so. It is a collection of many stories although one third occupies with the stories related to Mowgli. <br /><br />The most interesting ones in the book are the stories of Mowgli. And equally interesting was the story of the cute little mongoose, Rikki Tikki Tavi. He reminded me of the friendly mongoose who used to roam the garden of my childhood home. Other than them, the rest of the stories didn't grab me. They were alright but not quite interesting. And some of these other stories entertained certain disturbing notions and actions that might have contributed to my lesser enjoyment of them. <br /><br />When I read <i> Kim</i>, I felt that Kipling was not a good storyteller. <i> The Jungle Book </i> qualifies my feeling to some extent since I enjoyed the stories of Mowgli and Rikki Tikki Tavi. But I'm not quite sure if I want to read any more of his prosaic works.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5307800589527439456" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58009145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58009145" />
    <a title="Kristen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1116185-kristen"><img alt="Kristen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241138579p2/1116185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58009145?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristen" href="/user/show/1116185-kristen">Kristen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1116185-kristen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1116185-kristen?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1116185-kristen?shelf=1001-books-before-you-die">1001-books-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1116185-kristen?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1116185-kristen?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58009145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4196393137730520035">No this is not your Disney movie - Kipling wrote a fantastic series of short stories, only a few of which include Mowgli. Baloo is not a lazy idiot, Kaa is not a bad guy, SherKahn is killed rather then run off, the wolves are not always the noble good guys... this is VERY different then our cotton candy Disney film. And so much more enjoyable for it.<br/><br/>Also included are the tales of Rikki Tikki Tavi, the Elephant Dance, and the White Seal. I would have to say that my favorite is Rikki Tikki Tavi, h</span>
  <span id="freeText4196393137730520035" style="display:none">No this is not your Disney movie - Kipling wrote a fantastic series of short stories, only a few of which include Mowgli. Baloo is not a lazy idiot, Kaa is not a bad guy, SherKahn is killed rather then run off, the wolves are not always the noble good guys... this is VERY different then our cotton candy Disney film. And so much more enjoyable for it.<br /><br />Also included are the tales of Rikki Tikki Tavi, the Elephant Dance, and the White Seal. I would have to say that my favorite is Rikki Tikki Tavi, he's just so loveable and wonderful. There is a sense of magic in Kiplings animals, every reader will find themselves dreaming of being raised by wolves and taught the law of the jungle by Baloo.<br /><br />I know that this was written as a children's book - but I have a hard time believing that my 11 year old son would be able to read and enjoy it, the beauty of the language will be lost on many of the younger readers who are used to the forward speaking newer YA books. But I would assume that by middle school they should be able to fully enjoy it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4196393137730520035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1721384972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer374857642442368914"><b> PRE - READ. </b><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1470544344ra/19939409.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>...as he believes I read too much Abs books *wrinkles nose*<br/><br/>Commencing on the 8th of August (9 Aug - Aus time)<br/><br/>>>>>>>>>>>>>>><br/><br/><b> POST-READ </b><br/><br/>EDITED FOR <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1722851444?comment=156530787#comment_156530787' rel='nofollow noopener'> Luke's review link.</a> <br/><br/>This is the second time I read The Jungle Book. The first one was when I was in secondary school as I had to write an essay for it.<br/><br/>This story was memorable coz it was the same time when I first start wearing glasses. So, needless to say this was the very first story I read with my pair.<br/><br/>Back then though, you were conside</span>
  <span id="freeText374857642442368914" style="display:none"><b> PRE - READ. </b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1470544344ra/19939409.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>...as he believes I read too much Abs books *wrinkles nose*<br><br>Commencing on the 8th of August (9 Aug - Aus time)<br><br>&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&gt;<br><br><b> POST-READ </b><br><br>EDITED FOR <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1722851444?comment=156530787#comment_156530787" rel="nofollow noopener"> Luke's review link.</a> <br><br>This is the second time I read The Jungle Book. The first one was when I was in secondary school as I had to write an essay for it.<br><br>This story was memorable coz it was the same time when I first start wearing glasses. So, needless to say this was the very first story I read with my pair.<br><br>Back then though, you were considered/teased/called as a nerd when you wear spectacles. <br>The bigger the frames, the bigger the lenses, the nerdER you are.<br>So, you can just imagine my hesitations and disappointment when I've been told I needed a pair.<br><br><br>Nowadays, every second person I see wears them.<br>Glasses became a fashion statement. <br><b> <u>Glasses make a big fashion statement. </u> </b><br><br><br>So, back to the book. <br>When Luke asked me to BR this classic, I said yes to it, of course.<br><br>When I got my copy though, I had to double check the cover. <br>The book was thickER than I expected.<br>And then after reading "Mowgli's Brothers" I was surprised that there were more pages to turn. <br><br><i> Huh? </i> <br><br>So then I realised that the book consists of more short stories and poems.<br>Though, only three or four of them are actually stories about Mowgli, Baloo, Bagheera aka Baloogheera (as Luke cleverly put the two names together) and Shere Khan. <br><br>Alrighty then, read more, I did.<br>{Side comment: I think my teacher has provided the class a "photocopy" of Mowgli's Brother story only)<br><br>I liked all of the stories and they all fit together okay as Kipling showcased animals.<br>Animal fables to which the author explored themes such as courage, loyalty, friendship and love. But my favourite is "Riki-Tiki-Tavi". That mongoose was adorable and his loyalty &amp; love for the family who took him in were boundless and admirable.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1472738315i/20235691.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i> Love lovelovelovelove this character. </i><br><br>I'm not gonna go into details as I'm sure everyone has read this. <br>*grumbles* am I the only one here who was surprised that it's a collection of poems and songs?!<br><br>But what I'll do in this review of MOWGLI's STORY (only) is to compare what I read and what I watched.<br><br><u>The Book.</u><br><br>The writings was old and there were parts that made me either bored or had to stop and absorb what Kipling was trying to say.<br><br><br>Baloo was very wise, teaching Mowgli almost everything. He was very strict and always ready to strike a blow on young Mowgli when he doesn't pay attention or get anything wrong.<br><br><i>“Better he should be bruised from head to foot by me who loves him than that he should come to harm through ignorance,” Baloo answered very earnestly.” </i><br><br>Bagheera, who is another companion of Mowgli and a wise one too.<br>I forgot about his story. His secret bald spot and that mark. The collar mark that no one knew.<br><br><i> “And yet, Little Brother, I was born among men.<br>Fed behind bars from an iron pan till one night I felt that I was Bagheera—the Panther—and no man’s plaything, and I broke the silly lock with one blow of my paw and came away.” </i><br><br>And Kaa. Interestingly enough as I couldn't remember, as almost everything in this book, that he was an ally too who helped Mowgli to escape from those rambunctious monkeys. <br><br><u>The difference.</u><br>These 3 main characters were nowhere near anything like this in the film. <br>They were fun and very entertaining. <br>Never felt that I was watching an adaptation of a classic story. Disney film had changed the plot enormously. And even added a new character.<br><br>One other thing I noticed though, was that Mowgli didn't have a female influence on his side. <br>Sure he had mother wolf but she had a very short role and did nothing really heroic.<br>It makes me think that perhaps, this was the reason why Kaa, the python was voiced by a female actress in the new film to balance the gender equation that influenced young Mowgli.<br><br><i> Could it be? </i><br><br>The other difference. <br>Over the years, I managed to collect 4 pairs of glasses (2 of em are useless; prescription-wise) and have earnt myself another pair recently.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1472738315i/20235692.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Also, reading it this time around is not because of school requirement but just for the sake of reading and enjoying it as it should be.<br>And what makes it a spectacular read too is I BR it with a very good friend.<br><br><s>Now, I will edit this review when Luke had finished and posted his review.<br>His review link will be added as I'm sure his thoughts will be thorough.<br>After all there's a reason why he is <br>the writer and I, I use that other pair of glasses I just acquired. </s><br><br>Rating it <b>3.5</b> <i> Baloogheera-for-the-win </i> <b>Stars</b> <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1472740400ra/20236011.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

  <span id="freeText11355129617992558057" style="display:none">Due to watching the Disney movie of "The jungle book" I assumed that this book was going to be entirely about Mowgli, but to my surprise, I discovered quite a few different tales and poems.<br />I liked that all of the stories in this book showcased animals, and the themes of love, loyalty and friendship were deeply explored. That is never a good sign for me, and it is that that has made this book a lower rated one. <br />Personally, I don't think that all children now, would appreciate the style of writing and the setting like an adult would. I'm glad I've read this classic, but for me, it definitely isn't a masterpiece. </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781626862586" href="/book/show/20893982-the-jungle-book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer5609474681153638474">This holds up surprisingly well for something that could have been full of English racism back in the good old days of 1894. Indeed, some of the stories read very much like a modern YA book of parables with animals who seem much more human than the humans. <br/><br/>Big surprise, right? We humans are a monstrous lot. <br/><br/>That being said, this isn't just the source of Disney's Jungle Book, although a part of it is. It's also full of other great stories. Most memorable is Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, the mongoose who kill</span>
  <span id="freeText5609474681153638474" style="display:none">This holds up surprisingly well for something that could have been full of English racism back in the good old days of 1894. Indeed, some of the stories read very much like a modern YA book of parables with animals who seem much more human than the humans. <br /><br />Big surprise, right? We humans are a monstrous lot. <br /><br />That being said, this isn't just the source of Disney's Jungle Book, although a part of it is. It's also full of other great stories. Most memorable is Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, the mongoose who kills a cobra. But also horrifying is the tale of the sea cows, or rather, the White Seal. The tale of the elephant, Toomai, was delightful.<br /><br />I should say it would be a great book for any child, but not many modern children know crap about India except, in my daughter's case, some snappy tunes and dance moves. The connect is kinda missing, you know? Like... Tarzan? Who the F*** is he?<br /><br />Even so, as an ADULT, it's rather charming and delightful. :)</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_115571803" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115571803" />
    <a title="Amber Tucker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3772259-amber-tucker"><img alt="Amber Tucker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307125204p2/3772259.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115571803?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amber Tucker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amber Tucker" href="/user/show/3772259-amber-tucker">Amber Tucker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780333361450" href="/book/show/2126651.The_Jungle_Book">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">hardcore children's lit fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3772259-amber-tucker?shelf=why-is-this-classic-again">why-is-this-classic-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3772259-amber-tucker?shelf=disappointments">disappointments</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115571803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9051838549006225760">Lesson learned from this book: having been much- and long-beloved does not automatically make a book worth reading. <br/>The only particular reason I picked this one off my shelf was the feeling it's a "classic" of children's lit, which I felt slightly ashamed of never having had a chance to enjoy – I assumed must be classically marvellous. (I mean, I don't know if I ever even watched the Disney adaptation all the way through. I was actually expecting <i> all </i> Mowgli stories. More than half are not, act</span>
  <span id="freeText9051838549006225760" style="display:none">Lesson learned from this book: having been much- and long-beloved does not automatically make a book worth reading. <br />The only particular reason I picked this one off my shelf was the feeling it's a "classic" of children's lit, which I felt slightly ashamed of never having had a chance to enjoy – I assumed must be classically marvellous. (I mean, I don't know if I ever even watched the Disney adaptation all the way through. I was actually expecting <i> all </i> Mowgli stories. More than half are not, actually, about Mowgli in any way.) What I was sorry to find, however, is that these stories are just classically bad. In its own way, this is frankly one of the weirdest books I've ever read. Even its age doesn't pardon it, in my opinion. <br /><br />One of my first thoughts, rather unaccountably it may seem, was "This reads like the Bible." This isn't wholly a negative thing. For example, I recognize and appreciate the often-poetic language used in the Bible, and I can do the same here. The little songs and poems between the chapters are metrically perfect (this counts for a lot, with me) and if not beautiful, are nicely-formed; in form and language, they always evoke the characters he's created for the animals. Here are a couple verses of the <i> Song of the Bander-log</i> [monkeys:]:<br /><br /><i> Here we sit in a branchy row,<br />Thinking of beautiful things we know. <br />Dreaming of deeds that we mean to do, <br />All complete, in a minute or two-<br />Something noble and wise and good, <br />Done by merely wishing we could.<br />We've forgotten, but- never mind, <br />Brother, thy tail hangs down behind! <br /><br />All the talk we ever have heard,<br />Uttered by bat or beast or bird-<br />Hide or fin or scale or feather -<br />Jabber it quickly and all together! <br />Excellent! Wonderful! One again!<br />Now we are talking just like men!<br />Let's pretend we are – never mind, <br />Brother, thy tail hangs down behind! <br />This is the way of the monkey-kind. </i><br /><br />See? It's cute... the charm fades, though, with re-reading. <br /><br />Now for what I really dislike. Two words: imperialism and anthropocentrism. Actually, anthropomorphism as well, but that can hardly be avoided, to a degree, so I'll let it go. <br />The 'best' of the various animal characters speak like noble British subjects (of about the sixteenth century, no less – people in Kipling's day didn't even talk like this – "thee-thy-thou, overformality considering we live in a jungle" etc, it's maddening), they have their own strict Jungle Law, and they call themselves people. Could it be any more obvious that these animals are meant to represent humans? Furthermore, that the non-humanlike animals are fated by nature to kowtow to the more 'civilized' species? Too obviously allegorical = another commonality with parts of the Bible. This kind of attitude may be common enough in folktales and mythology, but doesn't excuse the gross colonial bias with which the whole jungle universe, both inhabitants and organizing principles, are presented.<br /><br />The animals respect, virtually worship, the humans. Though nearly all the characters are animals, everything about the stories centers around humanity, that is the glorious courage, order and reason of the British empire. If that's supposed to be modern folktale, it's akin to religious brainwashing. Not in the stories it tells, but in the value judgements implicit within these stories. And by the way, also like the Bible, I had to hate many of Kipling's 'heroes.' Little Toomai is a sneak and a traitor; I almost cried at the treatment of the elephants truthfully and matter-of-factly depicted in his chapter. Elsewhere: Rikki-Tikki made me want to stamp on a member of the family Herpestidae, pronto. I was rooting for the cobras. <br /><br />To conclude, if you're willing to accept a whack of disgustingly antiquated values threaded into not-especially-imaginative stories, you can enjoy <i> The Jungle Book. </i> I'm sorry that I couldn't find more to like in it. But it's safe to say, for once, that Walt Disney's job is probably better (at the very least, less offensive)!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9051838549006225760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1932778157" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1932778157" />
    <a title="Celeste" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste"><img alt="Celeste" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481055226p2/7317186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1932778157?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celeste" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celeste" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste">Celeste</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=childrens-books">childrens-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=classics-i-ve-read">classics-i-ve-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1932778157" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18396235716655072590"><b>Full review posted below.</b><br/><br/>This was my first book completed on the Serial Reader app, an awesome way to read classic works of literature in less than fifteen minutes a day. I highly recommend it to anyone who is interested in reading some classics, but who doesn’t want to get bogged down in them. And no, I haven’t been asked to advertise the app; I just really think it was a fantastic idea, and the execution of that idea was incredibly well done.<br/><br/>End advertisement. ;) Onto the story at hand.<br/><br/>Most ev</span>
  <span id="freeText18396235716655072590" style="display:none"><b>Full review posted below.</b><br /><br />This was my first book completed on the Serial Reader app, an awesome way to read classic works of literature in less than fifteen minutes a day. I highly recommend it to anyone who is interested in reading some classics, but who doesn’t want to get bogged down in them. And no, I haven’t been asked to advertise the app; I just really think it was a fantastic idea, and the execution of that idea was incredibly well done.<br /><br />End advertisement. ;) Onto the story at hand.<br /><br />Most everyone probably knows at least a little about this book, due in large part to Disney’s animated movie and their more recent live-action film. I enjoyed reading about Mowgli and his adventures growing up as the lone man-cub in the jungle. Bagheera the Panther, Baloo the Bear, and Kaa the Python all had different personalities than their film counterparts, but were just as much fun to read as they are to watch. Mowgli was headstrong and clever and never backed down from a challenge. Raised by a Wolfpack against the wishes of Shere Khan, the man-eating Tiger, Mowgli lived an interesting life to say the least. He learned every language present in the jungle, and then spent some time in a human village and learned to speak as they speak. But the village could not hold him. He conquered his foes and returned to the jungle, triumphant.<br /><br />Besides the main story of Mowgli, Kipling also included the stories of Kotick, the White Seal; Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, the Mongoose; Toomai, the Elephant boy; and different animals in the military, who argue about whose method of fighting is right. Of these, Rikki-Tikki-Tavi’s story was by far the best. I completely understand why his is the segment included in so many literature books, because it was the most engaging story in the entirety of the Jungle Book, in my opinion. I enjoyed the adventures of the little mongoose even more than I did the tales of Mowgli the man-cub. Second-best out of these secondary tales was the story of Kotick, the White Seal. I was thrown by his story at first, because it was the first after Mowlgi’s story, but once I adjusted to the change I enjoyed the little white seal, out to save his people from being butchered. He swam to the beat of his own drum, and I can always respect that. <br /><br />The last two stories weren’t enjoyable to me. They’re where I bogged down and just had to make myself power through to the end. I found Toomai annoying, and I could care less about which animal thought they were the most important in a battle. If the book had ended after the tale of Rikki-Tikki-Tavi, I would’ve easily given it 4 stars. But, because of the drudgery of the last two stories, I’m settling at a 3 here. It was a short, mostly fun classic to mark off of my “to-read” list, and I enjoyed marking it off in the 24 episodes that Serial Reader provided. The novelty of the app added to my enjoyment, and I will most definitely be reading more classics this way!<br /><br />For more of reviews, as well as my own fiction and thoughts on life, check out my blog, <a>Celestial Musings</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="18396235716655072590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_472611418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/472611418" />
    <a title="Terry " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1262683-terry"><img alt="Terry " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316628758p2/1262683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/472611418?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terry " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terry " href="/user/show/1262683-terry">Terry </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=ya-children-s">ya-children-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=audio-books">audio-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer472611418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14606766907126428709">Rudyard Kipling’s _The Jungle Book_ is an enjoyable read. A collection of short stories, all of which revolve around the lives and troubles of different animals and the people who interact with them, it has a surprising amount of depth coupled with rather pleasant prose. The most famous of these stories are probably those that revolve around Mowgli, the jungle boy raised by wolves in India whose adventures with Baloo the bear and Bagheera the panther against the machinations of Shere Khan the ti</span>
  <span id="freeText14606766907126428709" style="display:none">Rudyard Kipling’s _The Jungle Book_ is an enjoyable read. A collection of short stories, all of which revolve around the lives and troubles of different animals and the people who interact with them, it has a surprising amount of depth coupled with rather pleasant prose. The most famous of these stories are probably those that revolve around Mowgli, the jungle boy raised by wolves in India whose adventures with Baloo the bear and Bagheera the panther against the machinations of Shere Khan the tiger are fairly well-known (even resulting in a typically watered-down Disney movie from many years ago).<br /><br />All of the stories are notable for their fairly even handed treatment of the interactions between animals and men. The tragedy and pathos of the tribulations and abuse animals often have to suffer at the hands of man are not glossed over, but neither is it implied that all interactions between mankind and the animal kingdom are destructive or unwarranted. The animals are presented as having languages and customs of their own and Kipling generally does a pretty neat trick of managing to straddle the line between having his animal characters behave too much like humans and having them fall into unrelatability by being purely ‘animals’. The most significant contravention of this occurs, I think, in the story “Her Majesty’s Servants” in which, in my opinion, a group of animals serving various roles in a British regiment shade a bit more towards taking on the roles of their all-too human handlers. That quibble aside I enjoyed these morality fables and adventure stories, with those centring on Mowgli and his lessons in the Laws of the Jungle topping the list. Good clean fun with enough meat to the bone to give you something to think about.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14606766907126428709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
